To show the last part of the proof of Theorem 3.7, we will first state the following
claims which are needed for the last part.
Claims 1
I) Any three corner pieces can be permuted in a 3-cycle, while the rest of the
cubies are having their position and orientation preserved.
II) Any three edge pieces can be permuted in a 3-cycle, while the rest of the cubies
are having their position and orientation preserved.
III) Any two corner pieces and any two edge pieces can be permuted in a 2-cycle
respectively for the corners and for the edges, while the rest of the cubies are
having their position and orientation preserved.
These claims will be proven and we will show an example of each claim, before we
continue with the proof of Theorem 3.7.
Proof of Claims 1:
For each of these claims we must show that there exists a move
which permutes the cubies as stated. For Claim I) and II) we will use brute force to
find some the wanted moves. For Claim III) Method 2 will be described and can be
used to find the wanted moves.
When using brute force to show Claim I) and II), we are looking for all possible
3-cycles where the cubie in position P1 is always a part of the 3-cycle. Because the
move of the found permutation which include P1 can be rotated to include any of the
other cubies. As a reminder, position P1 for the corner is shown at Figure 3.4A and
at Figure 3.11A for the edge.
==PAGE:74==
66
3 Theory for Rubik’s cube
Brute force of Claim I)
There are
(
8
3
)
= 56 possibilities to choose 3 corners without replacement to be per-
muted. Each one of these possibilities have two permutations, one for each way to
permute giving a total of 112 permutations. To make the problem more simple, we
only look for the permutation of the combination of 3 corners where the corner at P1
is always chosen. There are only
(
7
2
)
= 21 different way to choose 2 of the remaining
corners. We choose to search for 21 permutations, one for each set of corners where
the permutation (P1 Pi Pj) have i < j. If we can find these 21 permutation with a
corresponding move, the 21 inverse permutations can be found just by making the
inverse move. To find the remaining of the 112 permutations we can apply rotations
of the found 42 moves.
The approach of finding the 21 permutations will be to use a 3-cycle move, which
permutes only three corners in same the layer while preserving the edge pieces and
rest of the corner pieces position and orientation. This 3-cycle move can be rotated 3
times to include the different corners in the same layer. If the inverse move is included,
there are 8 ways of permuting 3 corners in the same layer. These 8 moves can be
rotated such that the move will permute corners in a different layer. Therefore if one
moves are known, it can be inverted and rotated to the 6 different sides, giving a total
of 48 different ways of ’permuting 3 corners in one layer’. All these 48 permutations
is each saved with a number i from 1 to 48.
To start finding some of the 21 permutations, we will check if any of the 21 permuta-
tions is equal to one of the 48 permutations. If there is, then the given permutation
should be denoted with the belonging number of that permutation, i. The remaining
of the 21 permutations not denoted with any number, will be checked if they are
equal to a composition of two of the 48 permutations. If any of the 21 permutations
are found, then each of them will be denoted with the corresponding two numbers
belonging to the 48 permutations, which gave rise to the found permutation. We
continue to look at the composition of k of the 48 permutations with k = 3, 4, ...,
until all 21 permutations are found.
It is possible to find all the 21 permutations as a composite of 3-cycles, since they are
even and all even permutation can be written a composition of 3-cycles according to
Theorem 2.12. There are only |A8| = |S8|/2 = 8!/2 = 20160 elements in A8 it should
therefore be possible to find all the wanted 21 permutations by brute force, even if
we end up calculating all elements in A8 before finding the last wanted permutation.

)
= 55 different ways to choose the 2 remaining edges. We choose to search for 55
permutations one for each set of edges where the permutation (P1 Pi Pj) have i < j.
If we can find these 55 permutations with a corresponding move, the 55 inverse per-
mutations can be found just by making the inverse move. To find the rest of 440
permutations we can apply rotations of the found 110 moves.
We use the same approach as with the corners.
Here we want the 55 permuta-
tions as a composition of 3-cycles. It should be possible to find all the 55 permu-
tations as a composite of 3-cycles, because the 55 permutations are even and in
accordance to Theorem 2.12 they can be written as a 3-cycle composite. Though
for the edges we are working in a larger group than for the corners. Here we have
|A12| = |S12|/2 = 12!/2 = 239500800 elements in A12. It should be possible to use
brute force, but it may take more time than with the corners.
The 3-cycles are made by a move which permute 3 edges in the same layer, and
preserves the position and orientation of the rest of the cubies. From the one move
we can make 8 different permutations in one layer, which give 48 different ways of
’permuting 3 edges in one layer’ along the 6 sides.
We use one of the moves from the method solving the final layer as described in
Section 3.3. The move is
move E = M
(
R2uFbR2BfuR2)
,
which has the permutations of edges at position (P1 P6 P9). As seen in Figure 3.30
this move will preserve the orientation and position of all other cubies.

3.2 The Rubik’s cube group
69
The permutation of move E and move E−1 has been rotated several times to create
the 48 permutations, each denoted with a number i. By using brute force up to k = 5
we found 8822638 unique permutations before all of the 55 permutations were found.
As the unique permutations we had:
• 48 unique permutations consisting of a single 3-cycle. 2
• 1411 unique permutations consisting of a composition of two single 3-cycles.
• 32912 unique permutations consisting of a composition of three single 3-cycles.
• 619919 unique permutations consisting of a composition of four single 3-cycles.
• 8168348 unique permutations consisting of a composition of five single 3-cycles.
From the unique permutations we found:
• 6 of the 55 permutations which consist of a single 3-cycle.
• 0 of the 55 permutations which consist of the composition two single 3-cycles.
• 42 of the 55 permutations which consist of the composition three single 3-cycles.
• 5 of the 55 permutations which consist of the composition four single 3-cycles.
• 2 of the 55 permutations which consist of the composition five single 3-cycles.
The 48 created permutations can be found in Appendix B.3 with the corresponding
move and number. The 55 different permutations can be viewed in Appendix B.4
with the numbers to compose this permutation. The numbers is denoted as nE(i) for
the i’th permutation of the 48 permutations.
When checking if every 55 permutations from Appendix B.4 were composed correctly
to the wanted permutation, it was observed that some compositions had a single
edge, which was not part of the permutation, where the orientation was not pre-
served. When this occurs, it shall be corrected by using a move which only change
the orientation of two edges, e.g. a rotation of move 5 from the proof of Theorem 3.7
part b. The two edges to reorient, should be the edge with an orientation error, and
one of the edges from the permutation.
Each of the 55 permutations is now assigned with a move, which only permutes the
given 3 edges while the rest of the cubies preserves their orientation and position. By
using rotation and the inverse move of these 55 permutation, all the possible 3-cycle
permutations can be found. Hence this proves claim II).

For showing the last claim, we first will show that there exist moves to permute any

cubies not part of the permutation should have their orientation and position pre-

get all the combination of a permutation of two pair of corners and edges, one must
rotate the moves in the [DU] axis to get the remaining moves.
To permute any pair of corners and edges in the same layer, a rotation in the [BF]

When choosing any pair of two corners and the edges, it can be observed that at least
a corner and an edge will always be in the same layer. This is because when choosing
the first corner then it is part of 3 layers, e.g. the corner piece at P1 is part of U-,
L- and F-layer. When choosing the second corner it will at most share 2 of the same
layers as the first corners - this is when the two corners are adjacent. This leaves only
a single edge pieces not to a part of any of the layers for the corners as seen at Figure
3.27. If we choose this single edge as the first edge piece, then no-matter which other
edge is chosen to be the second edge piece to permute with, then it will be a part of
one of the layers containing one of the corners.
Figure 3.27: The pink edge is the only edge which is not part of any layer containing a
blue corner. The corners are adjacent.
Using the following method it is possible to permute any pair of two corners and two
edges.

We have now proven and shown the three claims given in Claims 1, and will continue
on the last part of the proof for Theorem 3.7.
Proof of Theorem 3.7 - Part c):
To show the last requirement of T ⊆R we needs to
show that there exist moves so the permutation for the corners and the permutation
for the edges will always have the same parity.
We begin in a solved cube, here are the permutation of the corners and the edges in
initial permutation, which is an even permutation. The requirement holds.
We have shown in Claims 1, that we can make any even permutations for the cor-
ners and for the edges, since all even permutation can be written as a composition
of 3-cycles. We have the moves to make any 3-cycle of the corners, see the proof for
==PAGE:85==
3.2 The Rubik’s cube group
77
Claim I), and to make any 3-cycle for the edges, see the proof for Claim II).
In the proof for Claim III) we have shown that there exists moves, which makes the
permutations odd both for the corners and for the edge. This the move which per-
mute any pair of two corners and two edges.
Therefore we have moves where the permutation of the corners and the permutation
of the edges always will have the same parity. Hence we have shown the last part of
this proof.
Since the we have shown there exist moves for the three requirements in Requirement
1, we have shown T ⊆R and the requirements are therefore sufficient. This conclude
the proof of T = R.
□
It should be mentioned for the matter of proving the last part of Theorem 3.7, it
would have been enough to show that a single move exist to permute a pair of two
corners and two edges, since if they permutations of the corners and the edges are
odd, one can perform that move, and the permutations would be even.
By using Theorem 3.6 and Theorem 3.7, it follows that the index of the subgroup R
in S is
[S : R] = 12 .
This means we can answer the Sections 3.2 preliminary question: ”In how many
different ways can the cube be scrambled? - without any disassembling of the cube.”
The answer is
|R| = |S|
12 = 38 · 8! · 212 · 12!
12
≃4.3252 · 1019

to achieve in the Rubik’s cube. Some interesting elements could be a pattern. Below
is an example of if a pattern is possible to make in R.

3.3 How to solve the Rubik’s cube
79
3.3
How to solve the Rubik’s cube
In this section we will describe how it is possible to solve the Rubik’s cube when using
two different methods. The first method is using the moves constructed in the proof
of Theorem 3.7 and the proof of Claims 1. The second method is the usual method
on how to solve the cube.
This section also describes how God’s number is found. God’s number is the maximum
required numbers of face turns to return from any scrambled cube to the initial solved
cube.
3.3.1
Solving the cube using the methods from Section 3.2.6.1
In the proof of Theorem 3.7 and the proof of Claims 1 we constructed some moves to
change the orientation and the position of the cubies. These moves can be used to
solve the cube back to the solved state from any scrambled state. How, is described
in Method 3.
Method 3:
To solved any scrambled state of the Rubik’s cube, this method can be used.
1. Determine the permutations of the corners and the edges.
2. If the permutations are odd, perform a move which permute two corners and
two edges. The pair of two corners and two edges can be chosen arbitrary.
3. The permutations are now even. Write them as a composite of 3-cycles using
Method 1.
4. All the cubies must be permuted back to the initial permutation.
Perform
therefore the moves of the inverse permutations. The tables in Appendix B and
possibly rotations of the moves in the tables can be used to find the inverse
move for each of the 3-cycles.
It is arbitrary if we start permuting all the edge pieces or all the corner pieces
back to their identity permutation.
5. Since all cubies should be in identity permutation, the cubies shall have their
orientation back to 0. The moves move 1 to move 6 and rotations of these can
be used to reorient the cubies. Appendix C have an overview of the moves.
A reorientation back to the solved state is always possible because Requirements 1 a)
and b) is always satisfied.
==PAGE:88==
80
3 Theory for Rubik’s cube
Below is an example of how a randomly scrambled cube can be solved using this
method.

3.3.2
The usual way solve the cube
We will in this section describe the algorithms used to solve the cube. For each move
given, we will describe the permutation and orientation the move gives rise to. We
will use the same steps as in the official guide on how to solve the cube from Rubik’s
Brand company [Ltd10]. The moves is the ones, used when solving the cube myself.
In this section only, we allow rotations of the physical cube, this is so the moves or
also called algorithms in this section, can be used on some of the other cubies, but all
the moves is written where all the faces has the colors given in Figure 3.2. We have
the following: front is blue, right is red, up is yellow, back is green, left is orange and
down is white.
The general way to solve the 3x3x3 Rubik’s cube is to first make a white cross in the
D-layer and then get the corresponding corners to the D-layer in the correct position
and orientation. The middle layer is solved afterwards.
To solve the last layer, first a yellow cross in the U-layer is made, and then the corners
are oriented to have orientation 0. When the all cubie-faces in the U-layer have the
==PAGE:91==
3.3 How to solve the Rubik’s cube
83
same color, the corners is permuted to the right position, and lastly the edges are
permuted to the correct position. Now the cube should be solved. The steps described
is shown at Figure 3.38. When the goal of the step is constructed, go to the next
step.
Step 1
Step 2
Step 3
Step 4
Step 5
Step 6
Step 7
Figure 3.38: The goal of the 7 steps to solve the Rubik’s cube
Below we will go through each step in details. We will state which of cubies must have
the correct position and/or orientation to have the goal acheived. All the algorithm
mention below, state the permutations and orientation of the cubies after performing
the move. This is included to illustrate why these steps will solve the Rubik’s cube,
since all the algorithms will preserve the permutation and orientation of the previous
steps. Though in the last steps, the algorithms will preserve the yellow cubie-faces
given from the previous steps.
The permutation and orientation of a move is found using the constructed matlab
program, where the move is performed on a solved cube. The reason to use the solved
cube, is to easily identify which of the cubies will have their position permuted or
have their orientation changed. The algorithm will have the same effect on a solved
cube, as the state of the cube, which needs the given algorithm to obtain the goal of
the step, since for each state of the cube we can make a relabeling of ci and ei.
The relabeling will make the cubie at position P V

for the corners or P E

for the edges
to the i’th element in respectively ci and ei. Therefore the order of the orientation
vectors will be in the same order as the position. As a reminder the labeling of po-
sition and orientation can be seen at Figure 3.4A and Figure 3.6 for the corners and
at Figure 3.11A and Figure 3.12 for the edges.
Because we find the orientation in a solved cube, the given orientations in the ta-
bles, will be the orientation change of the given cubie, e.g. if the corner at position
P V
8 , has orientation 1 in the cube to be solved and v8 = 2 in the table, then after
the permutation the cubie which was at P V
8 will now have orientation 1+2 ≡0 mod 3.

3.3 How to solve the Rubik’s cube
93
3.3.3
God’s number
A questions one could ask is: ”For any given state of the cube, what are the maximum
of face turn before we have a solved cube agian?” This number is known as God’s
number.
A face turn is defined as using one of the generators of either the quarter turn metric
(QTM) or of the half turn metric (HTM). As mention in the introduction we have
the generators of the two metrics as:
QTM :
{F, f, R, r, L, l, U, u, D, d, B, b}
HTM :
{F, F 2, f, R, R2, r, L, L2, l, U, U 2, u, D, D2, d, B, B2, b}
In the group R we have used the quarter turn metric as the generator.
The approach used to determine God’s number is to create a lower and an upper
bound. This section describes how the lower and upper bounds have been found.
Using a counting argument, we will calculate a lower bound. We will start by defining
a distinct move as a way of obtaining a rearrangement of the cube by a sequence of
face turns.
This means the move M (RL) and the move M (LR) are counted as
two distinct moves, although the moves gives the same state of the cube. In Section
3.2.6.1 we found |R| = 8! · 38 · 12! · 212/12 ∼= 4.3252 · 1019. Using only a sequence of at
most n face turns in QTM, what is the value n to make at least |R| distinct moves?
If we can find n then we have found a lower bound of God’s number in QTM.
With n = 1 we can make 12 distinct moves. Using n = 2 face turns, we can create
12 · 11 distinct moves. We can only make 11 new distinct moves in the second face
turn, since we will not use the inverse of the previous face turn. With n face turns
we can make 12 · 11n−1 distinct moves.
Solving the following inequality, we can a lower bound for n:
12 · 11n−1 ≥8! · 38 · 12! · 212
12
⇒
n ≥⌈18.819⌉= 19
This gives a lower bound of God’s number to 19.
To determine a thigher lower bound for God’s number it requires a move to obtain
a state of the cube, where there do not exist any other sequences of moves with
fewer face turns to obtain the same state of the cube. In January 1995 Michael Reid
proved that the superflip position can not be solved in less than 20 HTM face turns
[Rok+10]. The superflip position is shown at Figure 3.55A, here all the cubies are in
identity permutation, where all corners have an orientation of 0 and all edges have
an orientation of 1. The move with 20 HTM face turn is given in Table 3.54 and it
can be observed to have 28 QTM face turns.
Michael Reid found a shorter move for the superflip in QTM, it can be made using
==PAGE:102==
94
3 Theory for Rubik’s cube
only 24 QTM face turns [Rei95] (or 21 HTM face turns). Jerry Bryan proved in
February 1995, that there was no shorter moves in QTM that could produce the su-
perflip [Bry95]. This gave 24 as a lower bound for God’s number in QTM.
In August 1998 Michael Reid proved that the superflip composed with fourspot, was
a position in the cube that required 26 QTM face turns, and that this position
could not be made using fewer QTM face turns, [Cub98, Sun Aug 2]. The move for
the superflip composed with fourspot is in Table 3.54 and shown Figure 3.55B. The
fourspot ”permutes” the center pieces (F B)(R L), but since the center pieces are
fixed, we must rotate the cube 180◦through the [DU] axis, so the permutation of the
center pieces can be observed in the figure.
26 is therefore a more tight lower bound for God’s number in QTM. In HTM the
lower bound is 20.

Moves M (·)

3.3 How to solve the Rubik’s cube
95
To obtain an upper bound it should be shown that all |R| ≈4.3252 · 1019 possible
combinations can be solved using at most x face turns.
This is computationally
expensive. Tomas Rokicki and his team used brute force to find all state of the cube
can be solved 20 face turn or less in HTM [Rok+10] and 26 face turns or less in QTM
[RD14]. More details about the brute force for HTM is described in their article
[Rok+13].
Since both a lower and an upper bound are established, the search for God’s number
is ended. God’s number is 26 in quarter turn metric, QTM, and 20 in the half turn
metric, HTM.
Since God’s number in QTM are 26, this means that if a move is longer than 26 face
turns in R, then there exists a move which does the same permutation and orientation
in lest than 26 face turns. The thesis will not cover how the smaller move can be found,
but a way is to use a solver like [Gru17]. To use the solver, one must first perform the
long move on a solved cube, and type in the color of each cubie. The solver will then
give the move of how to get back to the solved cube. By inverting this move, a shorter
move should be obtained as wanted. The solver aims for using as few steps as possi-
ble, but there is no guarantee that it will not give moves longer than 26 face turns in
QTM. An example of this solve can produce longer than 26 face turns in QTM is the
move M
(
FuF 2dBUrfLdruLUbD2rFU 2D2)
which is 20 in HTM and 24 in QTM.
[Gru17] produces the solution M
(
U 2RDburfLDBU 2rF 2uR2U 2dB2R2DL2uB2R2)
which is 24 in HTM and 34 QTM, the inverse move will have the same length.
==PAGE:104==
96
3 Theory for Rubik’s cube
3.4
Matlab programs about the cube
In this section, we will describe the developed features made in Matlab for this thesis.
We will describes how the cube is constructed, how face turns and orientation of
cubies is implemented and lastly how moves are rotated and inverted. In the last
part of this section we describes the methods and some thoughts about the brute
force algorithm used to proof I) and II) in Claims 1.
3.4.1
An overview of the code
All the code is included in Appendix D, and also attached in the zip-folder. The
zip-folder includes also the result from the brute force. All code is made by myself,
except for the struct ’permutation’ is from [Sch09], which is used in the calculations
for the brute force, and to easily read a permutation in the cycle format.
The code consists of a run file and four folders. The run file is in Appendix D.1.
The first folder is with some setup settings, see Appendix D.2, which include the
initialization of the colors and coordinates to construct cube. It is in this folder the
permutation and orientation for the face turns is defined, here both for the corners
and edges independently, but also the combined permutation for the all the cubie-
faces is included.
There is a folder with the functions used to apply a face turn to a cube, to display the
cube, to find a rotation or the inverse of a move, to help calculating the orientation
and lastly is a function to read a move from the command window. The files is in
Appendix D.3.
There have been made a few scripts to generate some figures for the report, these are
in the folder ’Script for images’, see Appendix D.4.
The last folder is the code for all the work with the brute force. There are scripts for
the corners and for the edges, these can be read in Appendix D.5.
3.4.2
Construction of the cube
Several figures is made using the matlab code. These are generated from either a
move, or by specifying the color of each of the cube-faces. Each of the cubie-faces in
the cube have a coordinate and a color. We have used a (n × n × ’6 faces’)-matrix
to contain the colors in the cube. To make indexing faster, we have denoted each of
the faces with an index, the color of the face have the same index, i.e. front = blue
= 1, right = red = 2, left = orange = 3, up = yellow = 4, down = white = 5 and
back = green = 6. The coordinate for a cubie-face is given by four 3D-points, all
the coordinates is saved in a (n × n × ’6 faces’ × ’3 axes’ × ’4 points’)-matrix, where
the cubie-face element (i, j, f) ∈(n × n × ’6 faces’) will denote the same cubie-face
in both matrices. To plot the cubie-faces we draw a filled square in the given color,
using the four points as the corners of the square.
==PAGE:105==
3.4 Matlab programs about the cube
97
We have made two structs cube_constant and current_cube to include all the de-
tails about the cube, and what is done to the current cube (the cube to have the
moves performed at).
The struct cube_constant contains:
• The size of the cube.
• Coordinates to each of the cubie-faces.
• The permutation and orientation of the face turns, for the cubie-faces, the
corners and the edges.
• The color scheme and a matrix containing all colors of the cubie-faces for the
solved cube.
The struct current_cube contains:
• The current color of all the cubie-faces.
• Position and orientations of the corners and the edges.
• The move and the inverse move from a solved cube to the current state.
If we set plot_initial_cube to 1 in the run-file, and put a break-point at line 65
(before create_faceturns) it is possible to plot any n × n × n cube. We have only
implemented face turns for n = 2 and n = 3.
3.4.3
Implementing of face turns and moves
We have use the fact that Matlab stores the elements of a matrix as a single vec-
tor of elements, called linear indexing, [Mat17]. As example the entry (i, j, f) in a
(d1 × d2 × d3)-matrix, will have entry (f −1)(d1 · d2) + (j −1)d1 + i in the vector. In
a (3 × 3 × 6)-matrix the element at (2, 1, 3) will have entry 20.
A face turn in the cube is implemented as a permutation of the entries in the matrix of
the colors of the cubie-faces, where the entries is given by the linear index. In Figure
3.56 the entry in the linear index is given for the six layers. In the code the face turn
is hard-coded by first defining the permutation of the corners and the permutation of
the edges, and afterwards composed into the face turn permutation. As an example
we have the permutation of the face turn F, first given as the cube-face position, and
afterwards as the linear index:
Fcorners = (ful fur fdr fdl)(udl ruf dfr ldf)(luf ufr rdf dfl)
= (1 7 9 3)(30 10 43 27)(25 36 12 37)
Fedge = (fl fu fr fd)(lf uf rf df) = (2 4 8 6)(26 33 11 40)
F = Fcorners ◦Fedge

Figure 3.56: The linear index number of every cubie-face at the six different layers.
Moves
In the run-file there is a variable enter_move_in_command_window if it is sat to one,
the move will be read as the input from the command window, else the move must
be written as a string in the code. The allowed face turns are
F, F2, f, R, R2, r, L, L2, l, U, U2, u, D, D2, d, B, B2 and b .
Spaces are allowed too.
The move is implemented by the function apply_faceturn_at_current_cube which
needs cube_constant, current_cube and the move as input, and the output is a new
updated current_cube. This function removes all spaces from the move, replaces all
2’s by the previous letter. The move is performed letter-wise by permute the cubie-
faces matrix with the colors. For each letter we will also update the orientation and
the labels for the positions of the corners and edges. We will also update the inverse
move letter-wise, since it is used for updating the orientation after the next face turn.
The inverse face turn is found using the function find_Xinv which take a face turn
as input, determine if we have a capital letter or not, and then respectively makes the
letter to a lower case or to a capital. Lastly the whole move is added to current_cube.
If we need the inverse of a move, without performing the move, we can use the func-
tion move_inv, which take a move (a string of face turns) as input. This function
==PAGE:107==
3.4 Matlab programs about the cube
99
replaces all 2’s with the previous letter. We use find_Xinv letter-wise again, but
must remember to place the inverted face turn in the correct (back-wards) order.
This function will respect spaces.
Sometimes rotations of a move is wanted. A rotation of the move can be found using
the function rotation_of_moves, which takes a move and a number from 1 to 9 as
input. The number represent the wanted rotation as
n :
1 = [BF]+,
2 = [BF]−,
3 = [BF]2,
4 = [LR]+,
5 = [LR]−,
6 = [LR]2,
7 = [DU]+,
8 = [DU]−,
9 = [DU]2.
The different direction of rotations is described in Section 3.2.4. The rotations is
implemented by adding the letter ’s’ behind every letter in the move, but not behind
any numbers, i.e. ’F’ becomes ’Fs’ and ’R2’ becomes ’Rs2’. The ’s’ is included to
insure we only permute a face turn once. Using the permutation from Table 3.17, we
replace ’face turn’-wise all the letters of the face turn ’X’ with the ’s’ attached, as
according to the permutation. The ’s’ is removed when the letter is replaced.
3.4.4
Some technical thoughts about the brute force
We will here shortly describe the algorithm behind the brute force. The variable wp,
cubie_pcs, max_k is fabricated only for this description, and _cubie is used instead
of _corners and _edges.
• First choose to use either the corners or the edges.
Set wp = 21, cubie_pcs = 8 and max_k = 2 for corners, and wp = 55, cubie_pcs
= 12 and max_k = 5 for the edges.
• Construct the unique 48 3-cycles where the moves is known.
Here we use the function construct_3_cycles_in_layer_cubie([a b c d]),
where the input [a b c d] is the cubies belonging in the given layer in a solved
cube. The function will then construct the known permutations in the given
layer.
• Add the 48 3-cycles to unique_cycles_cubie.
• Create a list of the wanted permutations list_all_pair_of_cubies_as_perm.
Each permutations is described as a vector, where the vector is second row of a
2 by n permutation matrix. This is to use the struct ’permutation’.
• Create the matrix: possible_3_cycles =
[list_all_pair_of_cubies_as_perm, zeros(wp, max_k), ones(wp,1)];
For each row in this matrix, the first cubie_pcs elements is the permutation,
the next max_k elements are to save the numbers of the 48 known 3-cycles
which will construct the permutation. The last element is to raise a flag if the
permutation is found, i.e. set the value to 0.
==PAGE:108==
100
3 Theory for Rubik’s cube
• Check if some of the wp permutations is some of the 48 known permutations.
If they are, then save the number, and raise the flag.
• Compose two 48 of the known 3-cycles, here using the struct ’permutation’ for
the calculation.
Save the two numbers to construct each new permutation and the permutation
itself.
• Check uniqueness of the combined permutations and save the unique. Of these
check for uniqueness with the permutations in unique_cycles_cubie.
Add the unique permutations with their numbers to the matrix
unique_cycles_cubie.
• Check if the wanted permutations, without a raised flag, is now a part of the
newly added permutations to unique_cycles_cubie.
• Continue composing the 48 known 3-cycles until all the flags are raised in
possible_3_cycles.
This method is implemented for the corner pieces, and a similar method is used for
the brute force of the edges. The final result for the edges is the same as using this
method.
There is made a few more scripts for the edges than for the corners, because we
began looking for same different patterns of the edges to be permuted, but the idea
was rejected in favor of the idea finding all permutations which included P E
1 . One of
the scripts corrects a small error, since it was faster than run the brute force again.
The run-time for brute force of the edges is a few hours at my laptop. The small
error was two swapped values in the input [a b c d] to construct the known 48 3-
cycles for the edges. The corrections of this was to reorder some of first 48 rows in
unique_cycles_edges.
For more about corrections error and the several scripts, see the comments in the
code where it is explained in details.
The result of the brute force of the 48 and 21 wanted permutations for the corners
and the 48 and 55 wanted permutations is included in the zip-folder with the cor-
responding move. Some of these move have the orientation error included, and is
not corrected in the file.
These are given in brute_force_result_edge.mat and
brute_force_result_corner.mat. These are used in Example 3.15. In these data
all the found 1163 permutations for the corners, with the corresponding numbers
is included.
In the data brute_force_result_edge_unique_perm.mat the found
8822638 permutations for the corners, with the corresponding numbers. These re-
sults are found in the sub-folder ’brute_force_result’.
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 4
Conclusion and
Possible extensions
In this thesis, we have used group theory to describe the Rubik’s cube. Here we has
focused on the 3x3x3 cube, consisting of 6 fixed center pieces, 12 edges pieces, also
known as the edges, and 8 corners pieces, known as the corners. Each of the 26 cubies,
have a unique coloring. When the cube is solved we have only one color in each side.
We do not allow rotations of the cube, i.e. we have linked the color together with the
corresponding face name.
We wants to use group theory to describe when we have an element from the sepa-
rated Rubik’s cube S, where disassembling is allowed, in the subgroup of The Rubik’s
cube group R, where only combinations of face turns are allowed.
Before we could describe the structure of the cube group we needed to introduce some
group theory. The most essentially concepts is the permutations, automorphism, semi-
direct product and wreath product. The theory of permutations and groups allows
one to describe very accurately the structure of the Rubik’s cube. Moreover, it gives
rise to a very explicit description of the group of rearrangements of the Rubik’s cube
generated by the face turns.
We have modelled the cube where any rearrangement can be described by the posi-
tion and orientation of the corners and the edges. The orientations are determined
by labeling, and the positions are described using permutations. The corners and
edges can be seen independently of each-other. We have made a sanity check of the
model, to be sure if we performed the same face turn four times, we will have the
same rearrangement in the cube as before we performed the four face turns.
The structure of the separated Rubik’s cube group is
S = (C3 ≀SV ) × (C2 ≀SE) =
(
(C3)8 ⋊ϕ SV
)
×
(
(C2)12 ⋊ψ SE)
where SV ∼= S8 and SE ∼= S12. An element from the group (S, ·) is
(
(v, r), (w, s)
)
,
where v ∈(C3)8 is the orientation vector, r ∈SV is the permutation of the corners,
w ∈(C2)12 is the orientation vector and s ∈SE is the permutation of the edges. The
==PAGE:110==
102
4 Conclusion and Possible extensions
binary operator · is defined as
(
(v1, r1), (w1, s1)
)
·
(
(v2, r2), (w2, s2)
)
=
((
v1 + v2P(r1), r2 ◦r1
)
,
(
w1 + w2P(s1), s2 ◦s1
))
where P(r) and P(s) are the permutation matrices for the permutations r and s.
The Rubik’s cube group R, must have the same face turn performed on both the cor-
ners and the edges,
(
(v(X), ρX), (w(X), σX)
)
. Here v(X), w(X) are the orientation
of the cubies after face turn X, and ρX, σX are the permutations of the cubies to
perform face turn X.
The generators of R are all the possible face turns in the quarter turn metric. From
investigating the properties of these generators and any combination of two elements
in R, we can deduce and proof the three requirements:
8
∑
i=1
vi ≡0 (mod 3)
,
12
∑
i=1
wi ≡0 (mod 2)
,
sign(r) = sign(s) .
We have shown these requirements are necessary and sufficient to determine if an
element from S is in R. This can be use to control if a wanted pattern is possible
to make. An element from S is fast and easy to determine if it belongs in the group R.
We have shown [S : R] = 12, where the proof is based on the requirement. This gives
|R| = |S|
12 = 38 · 8! · 212 · 12!
12
≃4.3252 · 1019
different rearrangements of the 3x3x3 Rubik’s cube.
As an addition to the structure of the group R, we have used permutations and the
orientation of the cubies, to show how it is possible to solve the Rubik’s cube.
It is possible to extend the knowledge about the structure of the 3x3x3 Rubik’s cube
to other cubes sizes. Here it must be possible to obtain similar requirements.
As example if we look at the 2x2x2 Rubik’s cube, known as the pocket cube, it
consist of only 8 corner pieces. The structure of the separated Rubik’s cube group
will therefore be S2x2x2 = (C3 ≀SV ). The subgroup R2x2x2 is where we only use face
turns to obtain rearrangement of the cube. Here we will use the same face turns as
for the 3x3x3.
The same three requirements from the 3x3x3, will also hold for the 2x2x2, tough since
there is no edges, the sign of the permutation of the corners can be both 1 and −1.
We are left with the requirement for the corners,
∑8
i=1 vi ≡0 (mod 3) .
==PAGE:111==
4 Conclusion and Possible extensions
103
This will be the only requirement of when an element from S2×2×2 is in R2×2×2.
Since there are no centers either, we will define some axes in the cube, so the cube
is only solved, when the colors is at the correct face, e.g. the blue face is at the
front, the yellow face is up, etc. Then we will have
[
S2×2×2 : R2×2×2]
= 3, from the
orientation requirement. This will give
R2×2×2 =
S2×2×2
3
= 38 · 8!
3
≃8.818 · 107
different rearrangements of this cube. Note that here rearrangements of a cube are
not identified with each other using any of the 24 rotational symmetries of the whole
cube.
This cube can be solved by using the same method as the 3x3x3, where one can use
step 2, 5 and 6 from the usual method.
If we want to investigate a bigger cube, one could chose the 4x4x4 Rubik’s cube, also
known as Rubik’s revenge. Before we can describe this cube, we must redefine the
face turns since we know have inner slice turns as well. One possibility would be to
use 2X to rotate the inner slice turn in the same direction as the face turn X, just
as in Figure 4.1A. A lowercase letter 2x is the inverse direction.
L 2L
2R R

(C)
Figure 4.1: (A) The inner slice turns, here the cyan inner slice rotated in the same direction
as R is denoted as 2R. Similar we have 2L for the magenta inner slice next
the L layer.
(B) One of the situations to occur in a even NxNxN cube. Two opposing edges
are swapped.
(C) Another of the situations to occur in a even NxNxN cube.
A edge is
flipped.
As in the 2x2x2, since we have no fixed centers, we will define some axes to fix the
position of the colors to the different sides. When we want to solve this cube, one
can first solve the centers in accordance to the defined axes. Then one must pair the
matching edges. Then the cube can almost be solves as the 3x3x3, except there are
two situations which can occur in the 4x4x4 and all other NxNxN cubes where N ≥4
is even, but not in 3x3x3 and all other odd NxNxN cubes. These two situations are
==PAGE:112==
104
4 Conclusion and Possible extensions
displayed at Figure 4.1B and 4.1C. These is called ’parity errors’ in the cubing world.
The ’parity errors’ are not errors in the parity as we have defined in the theory, but
just an expression. In accordance to [Ruw17], these ’parity errors’ occurs when we
are matching the edges, where two edges can be both be swapped and/or an edge can
be flipped. This is very difficult to avoid. The case with two swapped edges can be
solved with the move
M
(
(2R)2 U 2 (2R)2 U 2 (2U)2 (2R)2 (2U)2)
.
The case with a edge flipped can be solved with the move
M
(
(2R)2 B2 U 2 (2L) U 2 (2r) U 2 (2R) U 2 F 2 (2R) F 2 (2l) B2 (2R)2)
.
It must be a possible extension to deduce all the requirements for an element from
the separated Rubik’s cube group S4×4×4 to be in the Rubik’s cube group R4×4×4.
One requirement from the 3x3x3 which must still hold is
∑8
i=1 vi ≡0 (mod 3) .
Depending on how we interpreted the edges, either two matching edges as one unit,
or if have them as two units, we are not sure if the other two requirements holds. If
the edges is seen as one unit, we known the requirement for the orientation of the
edges will not hold, and also that the sign of the permutation of the corners can differ
from the sign of the permutation of the edges, because of the ’parity errors’.
According to [Wik17c], will the parity of the permutation of centers be equal to the
parity of the corners.
We have not found a structure of the separated Rubik’s cube group S4×4×4, but to
find the cardinality of this group we known there is 4 centers in each of the 6 sides, this
gives 24! of the different permutations of the centers, but if we assumes the centers
of the same color is indistinguishable, we can reduce the permutations with a factor
(4!)6 = 246. There is 24 edges, this gives 24! different permutations, and each edge
can be oriented in two ways, 224. Since two edges can be indistinguishable as well,
we can reduce the cardinality with a factor (2!)12 = 212. There are 8 corners, where
we have 8! permutations and 3 orientations for each corner, 38.
S4×4×4 = 24!
246 · 24! · 224
212
· 8! · 38 ≃2.183 · 1051
Using [Wik17c] we can deduce the cardinality of the Rubik’s cube group R4×4×4.
There are (8! · 38)/3 rearrangements for the corners, as also described in the 2x2x2
and 3x3x3. There are 24!/(246) rearrangement for the centers as in S4×4×4. [Wik17c]
claims the edges can not be flipped and that all permutations, odd and even, are
possible to obtain, this gives 24! rearrangements. Since we have fixed the axes, we
will not reduce the cardinality with a factor of 24 from the rotational symmetries of
the whole cube.
R4×4×4 = 8! · 38
3
· 24!
246 · 24! ≃1.776 · 1047 .
==PAGE:113==
4 Conclusion and Possible extensions
105
There are still some possible work to determine the accurately structure of the 4x4x4
Rubik’s cube. But when this is done, and maybe also for the 5x5x5 and even bigger
cubes, one could make an induction argument, to state the requirements to be in the
NxNxN Rubik’s cube, which may be split into the cases when N is even and when N
is odd.
Another possible extension, could be to look at the ’supercubes’. These are the cubes,
where there are images on the different sides to be solved as well. In the supercubes
can there be rotations of the centers, and in bigger cube, it is possible to distinguish
between the centers. In Figure 4.2 is shown an example on a 3x3x3 supercube and a
4x4x4 supercube.

Table B.3: The 21 wanted permutations for the corners.
The permutation is composed by nV (x2) ◦nV (x1). If the nV (x2) is empty use
nV (x2) = e, where e is the identity permutation.
When using this composition, orientation errors occurs at the corner position
given under ’Orientation error at’.
The inverse permutation of ’(i j k)
=
nV (x2) ◦nV (x1)’ can be found
by ’(i k j) = nV (x1 + a1) ◦nV (x2 + a2)’. Where ah = −1 if xh is even else
ah = 1 if xh is odd, for h = 1, 2.

apply_faceturn_at_current_cube.m
1 % function current_cube = apply_faceturn_at_current_cube(cube_constant ,
letters, current_cube)
2 %
3 % Apply the letters (as a string), on the current_cube.
4 % Remember cube_constant this is where the face turns are defined.
5 %
6 % This function includes the multiple features in current_cube compared to
7 % 'apply_faceturn.m'
8
9 function current_cube = apply_faceturn_at_current_cube(cube_constant ,
letters, current_cube)
10
% Make sure there is no space
11
letters =
letters(~isspace(letters));
12
13
% Replace F2 automatic by FF with use of
14
% https://se.mathworks.com/help/matlab/ref/isstrprop.html
15
if sum(isstrprop(letters,'digit'))>0
% letters include a digit
16
number2_index = strfind(letters,'2');
17
letters_before_2 = letters(number2_index -1);
18
for i = 1:length(number2_index)
19
letters = [letters(1:number2_index(i)-1),letters_before_2(i),
letters(number2_index(i)+1:end)];
20
end
21
end
22
23
% Do it letterwise
24
for i = 1:length(letters)
25
letter = letters(i);
26
% Permute the colors
27
current_cube.cube_color(cube_constant.perm.(letter)) = current_cube.
cube_color(cube_constant.idx.(letter));
28
29
% Permute the label
30
current_cube.corner_labels(cube_constant.corner_perm.(letter)) =
current_cube.corner_labels(cube_constant.corner_idx.(letter));
31
current_cube.edge_labels(cube_constant.edge_perm.(letter)) =
current_cube.edge_labels(cube_constant.edge_idx.(letter));
32
33
% Update the orientation
34
current_cube.corner_orient = mod(current_cube.corner_orient +
apply_orientation(current_cube.moves_inv,letter,'corner',
cube_constant),3);
35
current_cube.edge_orient = mod(current_cube.edge_orient +
apply_orientation(current_cube.moves_inv,letter,'edge',
cube_constant),2);
36
37
% Update the list of inverse moves
38
current_cube.moves_inv = [find_Xinv(letter),current_cube.moves_inv];
39
end
40
41
% Update the added moves
42
current_cube.moves = [current_cube.moves, letters];
43 end

rotation_of_moves.m
1 % function moves_after_rot = rotation_of_moves(moves_to_rotate ,n)
2 %
3 % Input:
4 %
moves_to_rotate (string)
5 %
Direction n (number from 1 to 9)
6 %
n: 1 = [BF]+ ,
2 = [BF]- ,
3 = [BF]2
7 %
4 = [LR]+ ,
5 = [LR]- ,
6 = [LR]2
8 %
7 = [DU]+ ,
8 = [DU]- ,
9 = [DU]2
9
10 function moves_after_rot = rotation_of_moves(moves_to_rotate ,n)
11
moves_after_rot = moves_to_rotate;
12
13
% The normal order of face turns
14
turns_before = {'F', 'R', 'L', 'U', 'D', 'B', 'f', 'r', 'l', 'u', 'd', '
b'};
15
16
% Make sure each of the letters in the move has an 's' attached
17
% (To distinguish which letters is changed)
18
for i = 1:12
19
moves_after_rot =strrep(moves_after_rot ,turns_before{i}, strcat(
turns_before{i},'s'));
20
end
21
22
% Use the correct 'key'
23
switch n
24
case 1 % [BF]+
25
turns_after = {'F', 'U', 'D', 'L', 'R', 'B', 'f', 'u', 'd', 'l',
'r', 'b'};
26
case 2 % [BF]-
27
turns_after = {'F', 'D', 'U', 'R', 'L', 'B', 'f', 'd', 'u', 'r',
'l', 'b'};
28
case 3 % [BF]2
29
turns_after = {'F', 'L', 'R', 'D', 'U', 'B', 'f', 'l', 'r', 'd',
'u', 'b'};
==PAGE:153==
D.3 Folder ’my_functions’
145
30
case 4 % [LR]+
31
turns_after = {'D', 'R', 'L', 'F', 'B', 'U', 'd', 'r', 'l', 'f',
'b', 'u'};
32
case 5 % [LR]-
33
turns_after = {'U', 'R', 'L', 'B', 'F', 'D', 'u', 'r', 'l', 'b',
'f', 'd'};
34
case 6 % [LR]2
35
turns_after = {'B', 'R', 'L', 'D', 'U', 'F', 'b', 'r', 'l', 'd',
'u', 'f'};
36
case 7 % [DU]+
37
turns_after = {'R', 'B', 'F', 'U', 'D', 'L', 'r', 'b', 'f', 'u',
'd', 'l'};
38
case 8 % [DU]-
39
turns_after = {'L', 'F', 'B', 'U', 'D', 'R', 'l', 'f', 'b', 'u',
'd', 'r'};
40
case 9 % [DU]2
41

'd', 'f'};
42
end

% Replace the letters with the given key, to make sure a letters
45

47
for i = 1:12
48
moves_after_rot =strrep(moves_after_rot , strcat(turns_before{i},'s')
, turns_after{i});
49
end
50 end

D.5 Folder ’brute_force’
155
D.5
Folder ’brute_force’
In this folder we have
• Brute force for corners
– brute_force_corners.m
– construct_3_cycles_in_layers_corners.m
– test_permutations_corners.m
• Brute force for edges
– brute_force_edges_no_1_of_4.m
– brute_force_edges_no_2_of_4_make_unique_cycles.m
– brute_force_edges_no_3_of_4_edge2appendix.m
– brute_force_edges_no_4_of_4_correct_order_of_48.m
– construct_3_cycles_in_layers_edges.m
– test_permutations_edges.m
• In the sub-folder ’brute_force_result’ we have the data-files with the results of
the brute force. These files is only included in the attached zip-folder:
– brute_force_result_corner.mat
– brute_force_result_edge.mat
– brute_force_result_edge_unique_perm.mat
Brute force for corners
